class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7790%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DFang%2BWan%252C%2BJin-Zhi%2BGuo%252C%2BXiao-Hua%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D12%26contentID%3Dacsami.5b12242%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BBinding%2BSb%2BNanospheres%2Bon%2BGraphene%2Bvia%2BOxygen%2BBonds%2Bas%2BSuperior%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BUltrafast%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7799%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2016" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0001.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Graphene incorporation should be one effective strategy to develop advanced electrode materials for a sodium-ion battery (SIB). Herein, the micro/nanostructural Sb/graphene composite (Sb-O-G) is successfully prepared with the uniform Sb nanospheres (∼100 nm) bound on the graphene via oxygen bonds. It is revealed that the in-situ-constructed oxygen bonds play a significant role on enhancing Na-storage properties, especially the ultrafast charge/discharge capability. The oxygen-bond-enhanced Sb-O-G composite can deliver a high capacity of 220 mAh/g at an ultrahigh current density of 12 A/g, which is obviously superior to the similar Sb/G composite (130 mAh/g at 10 A/g) just without Sb–O–C bonds. It also exhibits the highest Na-storage capacity compared to Sb/G and pure Sb nanoparticles as well as the best cycling performance. More importantly, this Sb-O-G anode achieves ultrafast (120 C) energy storage in SIB full cells, which have already been shown to power a 26-bulb array and calculator. All of these superior performances originate from the structural stability of Sb–O–C bonds during Na uptake/release, which has been verified by ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopies and infrared spectroscopies.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7790%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DFang%2BWan%252C%2BJin-Zhi%2BGuo%252C%2BXiao-Hua%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D12%26contentID%3Dacsami.5b12242%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BBinding%2BSb%2BNanospheres%2Bon%2BGraphene%2Bvia%2BOxygen%2BBonds%2Bas%2BSuperior%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BUltrafast%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7799%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292609&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanospheres" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanospheres" class="article__tags__link">Nanospheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony+Nanospheres&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony%20nanospheres">antimony nanospheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Oxygen+Bonds&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_oxygen%20bonds">oxygen bonds</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Full+Cells&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_full%20cells">full cells</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As one type of promising device for large-scale energy storage, the sodium-ion battery (SIB) has been considered as the optimal alternative for the traditional lithium-ion battery (LIB) due to its lower cost, more uniform distribution, and higher abundance of sodium resources in the earth’s crust. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1-5)</a> In the Periodic Table of Elements, sodium and lithium elements are located at the same Main Group, making both share the similar chemical and physical properties. This will facilitate the fabrication of practical and high-performance SIBs under the guidance of LIB development experiences. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6, 7)</a> However, the Na<sup>+</sup> radius (102 pm) is undesirably larger than that (76 pm) of Li<sup>+</sup>, which will make the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion as well as the concurrent electronic conduction much more difficult in the electrodes due to the more sluggish mass transfer process compared with the Li species. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8-10)</a> Hence, in order to develop superior SIBs, one of the main challenges should be to increase the ionic and electronic conductivities of key electrode materials to improve the Na-storage properties. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Because of the high theoretical capacity (about 660 mAh/g) and very appropriate Na-storage potential (about 0.5–0.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), metallic Sb material grabbed reseachers’ attentions in recent years. Many Sb-based materials had been previously prepared and studied, such as Sb hollow nanospheres, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Sb/C fibers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13, 14)</a> Sb/C nanocomposite, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15, 16)</a> Sb/graphene composites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Sb porous hollow microspheres, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Sb/MWCNT, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and Sb/acetylene black. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Although most of these composites exhibited excellent performance, the Na-storage properties need further enhancement. In recent years, two-dimensional (2D) graphene materials have been extensively employed as the structural building block to construct a highly conductive network, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 21 to 25">(21-25)</a> which can significantly enhance the Na/Li-storage properties of electrodes. This should be mainly benefited from the extremely high conductivity (theoretically 2.5 × 10<sup>4</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> V<sup>–1</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) of 2D graphene nanosheets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 26 to 29">(26-29)</a> For example, Yu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> have demonstrated that NASICON-type NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> anode material can deliver superior Na-storage performances in terms of high-rate capabilities and long cycle life after embedding active nanoparticles (NPs) into a three-dimensional (3D) graphene network. In addition to high electrical conductivity, the exceptional 2D flexibility of graphene nanosheets should be another significant structural characteristic to improve the electrochemical properties of electrode materials, especially the anode materials with high Na-storage capacity, because the huge volumetric variations caused by the Na uptake/release during cycling can be accommodated by the flexible graphene nanosheets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 30 to 33">(30-33)</a> Nevertheless, the composite on a physical level cannot achieve the highest Na-storage properties, because the severe aggregation of active NPs in the electrochemical sodiation/desodiation processes cannot be suppressed effectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref34" aria-label="reference 22 and 34">(22, 34)</a> Hence, it is still a big challenge to develop some new strategies to overcome the electrochemical aggregation of active NPs and thereafter improve the Na-storage properties of prepared graphene-containing nanocomposites.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we find that the in-situ-generated oxygen bonds between active nanospheres and the carbonaceous framework can significantly suppress the undesired aggregation of active Na-storage NPs, which is extremely important to enhance the Na-storage properties. In order to illustrate this breakthrough in the present paper, the metallic Sb nanospheres are chosen as the example of active material because of their high theoretical capacity (about 660 mAh/g) and very appropriate Na-storage potential (about 0.5–0.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) when used as anode for an SIB, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref17 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref17 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 14, 15, 17 and 35 to 37">(14, 15, 17, 35-37)</a> while in situ reduced graphene oxide (rGO) as the highly conductive 3D carbon framework. In the in situ preparation processes, Sb nanospheres were chemically connected with 2D rGO nanosheets via the strong Sb–O–C bonds due to the concurrent reduction of Sb(III) and graphene oxide (GO) species, forming the oxygen-bond-enhanced composites (thereafter abbreviated as Sb-O-G for convenience). The constructed Sb–O–C bonds not only prevent the electrochemical aggregation of active Sb nanospheres during Na-uptake/release cycling but also enhance the intimate contact between two constituents to realize a superior high-rate performance when used as anode materials for SIBs. Hence, the Sb-O-G exhibits much improved Na-storage properties, especially the ultrafast charge/discharge capability (about 220 mAh/g Na-storage capacity at a superhigh current density of 12 A/g), compared with the Sb/G (the similar material with Sb-O-G material just non-containing Sb–O–C bonds) and pure Sb nanospheres. In addition to the chemical interaction, the micrometer-sized secondary particulates centered at about 23.7 μm and appropriate nanopores in them have also played a significant role on improving the Na-storage properties. While the former can contribute to a high tap density of about 2.1 g/cm<sup>3</sup> to facilitate its practicability, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> the latter has the ability of accommodating the volumetric variations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Because of its above-mentioned comprehensive merits, the developed Sb-O-G anode was further assembled into full cells with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> These SIB full cells can deliver a high energy density of 160 Wh/kg at C/5 (completing the charge/discharge process in 5 h) and power density up to 7.1 kW/kg at 120 C (corresponding to an ultrashort charge/discharge time of 30 s).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Preparation of Sb-O-G Micro/Nanomaterial</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphene oxide <b>(</b>GO) was first prepared by oxidation of flake graphite using the improved Hummers method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> In a typical experiment for Sb-O-G preparation, 2.48 g of <span class="smallcaps smallerCapital">l</span>-tartaric acid was dissolved in 50 mL of deionized water, and then 0.57 g of SbCl<sub>3</sub> was added to the solution. Subsequently, the solution was diluted to 300 mL after adding 28.57 mL of GO aqueous solution (3.5 mg/mL). The diluted solution was further put in the ultrasonic bath for 20 min, and then added dropwise with 60 mL of NaBH<sub>4</sub> (0.01 g/mL) aqueous solution under continuous stirring. After another ultrasonic treatment of 3 h, a gray precipitate (Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial) could be easily collected by centrifugation, washed with deionized water and absolute ethanol, and then dried at 80 °C under vacuum.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of Sb/G</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Sb/G was prepared by annealing the Sb-O-G at 500 °C under a H<sub>2</sub>/Ar (5%:95%) atmosphere for 2 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Preparation of Sb Nanospheres</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The preparation processes were the same as those for Sb-O-G preparation except for the addition of GO solution.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Preparation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was prepared via the previously reported method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Typically, the molar ratio of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub>, H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was 2:3:1.5. H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> was added to the NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> aqueous solution dropwise under stirring; then Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and acetylene black (8%) were introduced into solution and stirred for 7 h at 70 °C. The solution was dried in an oven at 70 °C; after 4 h of being preheated at 300 °C, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> material was obtained through annealing at 800 °C for 8 h under an Ar atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed with a Rigaku D/max200PC (Cu Kα radiation). The particle size distribution profile was obtained by a Mastersizer 2000. An X-ray photoelectron spectrophotometer (ESCALABMKLL) from VG was used and the charge compensation of Al K Alpha. The Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) was obtained by a Nicolet 6700 spectrometer (Thermo) with a spectrum range from 7400 to 50 cm<sup>–1</sup> and a resolution ratio of &gt;0.09 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curve was taken from an Automatic Micropore and Mesopore Analyzer (ASAP 2020M). The TG curve was obtained from PE. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were from an SU8010 (HITACHI) and JEOL-2100 F, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The half cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (S-Universal 2440/750) from MIKROUNA using CR2032 coin cells. The NMP was added to the mixture of 80% active materials, 10% acetylene black, and 10% polyvinylidene fluoride to form a homogeneous slurry. The slurry was pasted on the copper foils with a mass loading of 2–3 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>. The electrolyte is 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate (EC/PC 1:1 by volume) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Separators were glass fibers (AH-934) from Whatman, and sodium metal was utilized as the counter electrode. The galvanostatic discharge–charge performances were obtained via battery testing systems (LAND CT2001A). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were carried out on a P4000 (Princeton Applied Research) with the frequency range from 1 MHz to 100 mHz. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were obtained from a VersaSTAT 3 (Princeton Applied Research) in a potential range of 0.01–2.0 V. For full-cell evaluation, the mixture of 70% of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, 20% of acetylene black, and 10% of polyvinylidene fluoride was used to prepare the cathodes. All the Sb-O-G anodes were first sodiated and desodiated for 1 cycle at 50 mA/g in half cells before be applied to full-cell usage.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Physical Characterizations of Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p">The chemical interaction of Sb–O–C bonds between Sb nanospheres (JCPDS No. 35-0732 as the X-ray diffraction patterns shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>A) and rGO nanosheets in the Sb-O-G material was first studied by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). In both XPS survey spectra (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>B,C) of Sb-O-G and Sb/G materials, there exist obvious peaks originating from Sb, C, and O elements without any other impurities. Usually, there is a thin oxide on the surface of Sb NPs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> By fitting the peaks at 543–526 eV, these peaks located at 539.8, 537.5, 533.1, 531.9, and 528 eV can be assigned to Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Sb-O, Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Sb<sup>0</sup>, O 1s of O-C, O 1s of O═C, and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Sb<sup>0</sup>; another peak located at 530.5 eV is the overlap of O 1s and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Sb-O (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>A). For the comparative Sb/G material (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>B), the peaks belonged to O 1s of O-C and O═C shift to the 533.7 and 532.4 eV, respectively. It is because of the existence of Sb–O–C bonds between Sb NPs and graphene in the Sb-O-G composite, which causes lower electron density at the O site and thus weakens the O–C and O═C bonds. In addition, the fwhm of the peak (O 1s and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Sb-O) in Sb-O-G is 1.3 eV, which is larger than that (1.1 eV) in Sb/G because of the additional existence of chemical bonds in Sb-O-G. Moreover, Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Sb<sup>0</sup> in Sb-O-G show lower intensity than those in Sb/G; this is since the existence of Sb–O–C bonds in Sb-O-G makes the Sb<sup>0</sup> become less in comparison to Sb/G. FT-IR measurement results (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>C) also verify such a strong oxygen-bond interaction between Sb nanospheres and rGO nanosheets in the Sb-O-G material because of the existence of an extra Sb-O-C vibration at about 598 cm<sup>–1</sup> compared with pure rGO and Sb/G material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42, 43)</a> Hence, both XPS and FT-IR analyses have confirmed the strong chemical Sb-O-C interaction between active Sb NPs and conductive rGO nanosheets in the prepared Sb-O-G material. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>D shows the schematic illustrations, which clearly illustrate the existence of chemical Sb–O–C bonds in the Sb-O-G material and no any interaction between two components in the comparative Sb/G material.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0003.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (A) The comparison of XRD patterns between the three samples (Sb NPs, Sb/G, and Sb-O-G micro/nanocomposites). The XPS spectra of Sb-O-G (B) and Sb/G (C) materials. The insets are the partially amplified curves in the dashed rectangle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0004.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. High-resolution XPS spectra of Sb-O-G (A) and Sb/G (B) materials in the range of 543–526 eV. (C) The comparison of FT-IR curves of rGO, Sb/G, and Sb-O-G samples. (D) The schematic illustrations which clearly compare the chemical interaction between Sb NPs and rGO nanosheets in the Sb-O-G and Sb/G materials.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the existing chemical interaction, the morphology and structure of the Sb-O-G material were further studied to clarify its superiority as anode for SIBs. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>A,B shows the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images at different magnification. As revealed, the Sb-O-G material is actually composed of tens of micrometer spheroidal micro/nanostructures, in which uniform Sb nanospheres (∼100 nm, transmission electron microscopy images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) are wrapped by flexible rGO nanosheets. Similarly, the Sb nanospheres in the Sb/G material are also dispersed in the 3D rGO network uniformly (SEM images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3A,B</a>). Additionally, the energy-dispersive X-ray spectrum (EDX) and elemental mapping (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>) further reveal that the key elements of Sb, C, and O are evenly distributed in both samples, demonstrating their excellent uniformity of elemental distribution. Further, as shown in the EDX (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>A,B), the oxygen content in the Sb/G is lower than that in Sb-O-G because of the thermal reduction. The particle size distribution (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>C) obtained from the laser diffraction method also confirms the micrometer-sized nature of secondary particulates centered at about 23.7 μm, which not only facilitate its practicability as anode materials for SIBs but also significantly increase the SIB’s volumetric energy density. Hence, the prepared Sb-O-G is actually typical micro/nanomaterial. More interestingly, there is an obvious hysteresis loop in the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curve (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>D), demonstrating the existence of mesopores in the secondary particulates. As the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) pore size distribution shows in the inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>D, the mesopores are mainly centered at about 47.2 nm. These mesopores can effectively accommodate the expansion/contraction of Sb nanospheres to improve the cycling performance. Furthermore, the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial is about 15.2 m<sup>2</sup>/g, which will be very beneficial to suppress the occurrence of electrochemical side reactions in the pure nanomaterials.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0005.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images (A, B), laser particle size distribution profile (C), and N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curve (D) of the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial. Inset of (D): the corresponding BJH pore size distribution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0006.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. EDX of Sb-O-G (A) and Sb/G (B). SEM image (C), and the corresponding elemental mapping images of Sb, O, and C elements (D–F) in the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Na-Storage Properties of the Sb-O-G Micro/Nanomaterial</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to illustrate the significance of chemical Sb-O-C interaction between active Sb nanospheres and the rGO conductive network in the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial as ultrafast anode for the SIB, the electrochemical Na-storage properties were first studied in half-cell systems. All the performances were also compared with the Sb/G as well as pure Sb NPs, which was prepared under the similar conditions for the Sb-O-G preparation just without GO addition and composed of irregular Sb particulates of 0.5–1 μm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3C</a>). Note that all capacities were calculated based on the total mass of composite materials, and the Na-storage properties of pure rGO material were individually evaluated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. As illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>A, the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial exhibits the best Na-storage properties including the highest reversible capacity and superb high-rate performance compared to the other two samples. For example, the Na-storage capacity of the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial is about 550 mAh/g, which is higher than 500 mAh/g for Sb/G and 490 mAh/g for pure Sb nanospheres. At a high current density of 2 A/g, the Sb-O-G can deliver a reversible capacity of up to 415 mAh/g, while the Sb/G and pure Sb NPs just keep the capacities of about 186 and 10 mAh/g, respectively. More interestingly, the Sb-O-G can still store Na with a capacity of about 220 mAh/g at the extremely high current density of 12 A/g. In the charge–discharge curves of the Sb-O-G as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>B, the plateaus attributed to the Sb alloying reaction are still visible at very high current density, demonstrating its superior high-rate performance. In comparison to many other Sb-based composites previously reported in high-quality journals, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref17 ref19 ref20 ref37 ref41 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref17 ref19 ref20 ref37 ref41 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 12 to 15, 17, 19, 20, 37, 41 and 44 to 47">(12-15, 17, 19, 20, 37, 41, 44-47)</a> as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the Sb-O-G exhibits the highest reversible Na-storage capacities when current densities are &gt;2 A/g among these reported Sb-based composites.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0007.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Na-storage properties in the half cells. The comparison of rate (A) and cycling (C) performances of the Sb-O-G, Sb/G, and pure Sb NPs. (B) The typical charge–discharge profiles of Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial at different current densities from 0.025 to 12 A/g, and (D) its CV patterns of the initial 5 cycles at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV/s between 0.01 and 2.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Additionally, the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial also exhibits the first-rate cycling performance as disclosed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>C,D. Although the non-rGO-containing Sb material becomes sharply inactive within 5 cycles, the capacity retention can be significantly improved after graphene incorporation. The Sb/G reversible capacity will stabilize at about 195 mAh/g after 200 cycles. Very remarkably, the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial can still maintain a high reversible capacity of about 460 mAh/g without obvious capacity attenuation after the same cycles. The superior cycling performance of the Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial is also verified by its cyclic voltammograms (CVs, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>D), in which the patterns become the same after 2 cycles. In the first cycle, the obvious reduction peak at 0.32 V can be attributed to alloying conversion of crystalline Sb nanospheres into the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phase, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and the reversible dealloying reaction reacts at about 0.9 V in the oxidation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> In the following cycles, the reduction peak transforms gradually into three at about 0.33, 0.50, and 0.65 V, which should be due to formation of hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, while the positions of oxidation peaks always maintain at about 0.9 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref35 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref35 ref48" aria-label="reference 9, 35 and 48">(9, 35, 48)</a> The similar changes of CV patterns are also observed for the Sb/G material, and there is no peak after one cycle for the pure Sb nanospheres because of its inactivation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> further compares the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) patterns of all three samples. It is clearly observed that two graphene-added materials (Sb-O-G and Sb/G) exhibit much smaller impedance than the pure Sb nanospheres, which indicates graphene significance to increase the composite electrical conductivity. However, both graphene-added materials have almost the equal impedance values, demonstrating that the increased conductivity is not the only factor of improving Na-storage properties, especially the extremely excellent rate performance. The chemical Sb-O-C interaction should be more significant because it can well stabilize the Sb nanospheres and prevent their aggregation during electrochemical sodiation/desodiation. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>A–F compares the SEM images of the Sb-O-G and Sb/G electrodes before and after cycles. As revealed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>B,C, the micro/nanostructures have still been preserved and Sb nanospheres can be clearly distinguished on the Sb-O-G electrodes after 200 cycles. In comparison for the Sb/G electrodes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>D–F), all Sb nanospheres have seriously aggregated and fused into bulks, causing the disappearance of micro/nanostructures after the same cycles.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0008.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrode SEM images of (A–C) Sb-O-G and (D–F) Sb/G materials: (A) and (D) are the fresh electrodes, and the others are the corresponding electrodes after 200 cycles. (G) The schematic of comparing the structural change of the Sb-O-G and Sb/G materials during cycling when used as anode for an SIB.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to make this viewpoint clearer, a schematic (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>G) is further drawn to compare the morphology and size changes of the Sb-O-G and Sb/G materials during cycling when tested as anode for an SIB. As shown, the Sb-O-G material can still maintain its micro/nanostructures without the aggregation of Sb nanospheres even after 200 cycles due to the existence of Sb–O–C bonds, while the Sb nanospheres easily detach with the rGO conductive network and self-aggregate seriously in the Sb/G electrodes. For the Sb-O-G anode, its excellent ability of micro/nanostructural preservation should be originated from the durability of existing Sb–O–C bonds, which had already been confirmed by ex situ XPS and IR tests. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>A,B, in comparison to the Sb/G composite, even after 200 Na-uptake/release cycles in the half cells, the O 1s peaks of O-C and O═C are located at 532.5 and 531.1 eV, respectively, which are still lower than 532.8 and 531.6 eV, respectively. This indicates that there yet are oxygen bonds between Sb nanospheres and rGO nanosheets in Sb-O-G. In addition, FT-IR measurement results (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>C) also verify that the Sb–O–C bonds still exist.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0009.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. High-resolution XPS spectra of Sb-O-G (A) and Sb/G (B) electrodes in the range of 543–526 eV after discharge/charge 200 cycles. (C) The comparison of FT-IR curves of Sb/G and Sb-O-G samples after 200 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Full-Cell Performance of the Sb-O-G Micro/Nanomaterial</h3><div class="NLM_p">For such an applicable research of energy storage, the practical application of as-prepared Sb-O-G micro/nanomaterial should be more significant than just declaring its superior electrochemical properties. Hence, its full-cell application was further studied by coupling with one high-performance Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-based composite (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) as cathode. In the half-cell charge–discharge curves of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and Sb-O-G anode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>A), there are excellent plateaus at 3.2–3.4 and 0.4–0.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, respectively, implying that the voltage output of Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells will center at about 2.7 V with an obvious plateau as already demonstrated by the tested charge–discharge curve (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>B). This is also confirmed by the obvious redox peaks in the CV patterns (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>C) of the same Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell. In addition to the charge/discharge ability of the SIB full cells, of more importance is their high specific capacity, extremely excellent high-rate performance, as well as good cycling stability. Note that SIB full cells used for performance evaluation were the soft-package ones (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figures 8">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>C–E) of about 3.5 × 6.5 cm<sup>2</sup> rather than the commonly used coin cells of small-type CR2032, and the mass loading in the anode and cathode was about 1.8 and 7.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, respectively. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>B,D, the full cells can deliver a discharge capacity of about 17.5 mAh (0.2 C). More excitingly, the full cells exhibit ultrafast charge/discharge ability (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>D), which is consistent with the evaluation result of ultrafast Na-storage property in half cells. For example, they can still discharge a capacity of about 8.2 mAh at a very high rate of 120 C (i.e., the charge/discharge process is completed within 30 s). In addition, the full cells also have good cycling performance with a capacity retention of about 78% after 50 cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>E), which can be further enhanced via optimizing the assembling processes of soft-package cells.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0010.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (A) The charge–discharge curves of both Sb-O-G and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrodes at 0.2 C in half cells; inset is the schematic illustration of Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell showing the charge and discharge processes. The performances of Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells: (B) charge–discharge curve at 0.2 C, (C) the initial five cycles of CV patterns at a scanning rate of 5 mV/s in 1.5–4.0 V, (D) high-rate capabilities from 0.2 to 120 C, and (E) cycling performance at 1.5 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the energy-storage performances, the practical applications of the Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells including coin and soft-package types were also demonstrated to power light-emitting diode (LED) bulbs and a calculator. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>A,B first shows the abilities of coin-type and soft-package-type full cells to lighten one single LED bulb, respectively, and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>C exhibits that the soft-package-type full cell can well power the calculator. More importantly and interestingly, the above 26 LED bulbs can be simultaneously lighted for above 1 h by one soft-package SIB full cell (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>D). All of these usage demonstrations imply that the Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells will exhibit extensive applications to power various electrical devices. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>E further shows the full cell’s Ragone plot to reveal the relationship between power and energy densities. As shown, its energy density has significantly reached about 160 Wh/kg (0.2 C), which is calculated based on the mass of active materials of both anode and cathode. The Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells have also exhibited a high power density up to 7.2 kW/kg (120 C), which should be mainly benefited from the ultrafast Na-storage capability of the Sb-O-G anode, as discussed above. The superior energy-storage performances with high energy and power densities are now pushing the present SIB full cells forward on the way of practical applications. Note that all SIB full cells presented here are monolithic ones, whose energy-storage performances could be further enhanced by optimizing the assembly processes under the guidance of mature LIB technologies.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12242d_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0011.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Application demonstrations of Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells: coin-type (A) and soft-package (B) cells lighten one single LED bulb, respectively; and one soft-package full cell powers a calculator (C) and lightens an LED array containing 26 bulbs (D). (E) The Ragone plot of Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells showing the relationship between energy and power densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/asset/images/large/am-2015-12242d_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12242&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, the micro/nanostructural Sb-O-G composite is successfully prepared with the uniform Sb nanospheres (∼100 nm) bound on the graphene via oxygen bonds. It is excitingly found that the in-situ-constructed oxygen bonds play a significant role on enhancing Na-storage properties of a high-capacity Sb anode, especially the ultrafast charge/discharge capability. At an ultrahigh current density of 12 A/g, the oxygen-bond-enhanced Sb-O-G composite can still deliver a high Na-storage capacity of up to 220 mAh/g. In addition, the Sb-O-G composite also exhibits superior cycling performance (almost no capacity decay after 200 cycles) and the highest Na-storage capacity of 550 mAh/g compared to 500 mAh/g for Sb/G (the similar material just without the existence of Sb–O–C bonds) and 490 mAh/g for pure Sb nanospheres. Furthermore, the micro/nanostructural nature of the Sb-O-G composite makes its tapping density up to 2.1 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, which will be very beneficial to practical application. Hence, its full-cell performances are further evaluated by coupling with one Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-based composite as cathode material. As expected, the Sb-O-G/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells exhibit ultrafast (120 C) charge/discharge property as well as high energy (160 Wh/kg at 0.2 C) and power (7.2 kW/kg at 120 C) densities. The superior energy-storage performances of such SIB full cells are now pushing them forward on the way of practical applications. In view of the universality of this concept, the enhancement for energy-storage properties via in-situ-generated chemical oxygen bonds, the present design could also be extended to other electrode materials of SIBs and LIBs. Presently, it has been preliminarily realized for a Sn anode, which is still in progress for deep study.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b12242" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b12242</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">The additional XPS spectra, SEM images, TEM images, TG curves, and electrochemical data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12242/suppl_file/am5b12242_si_001.pdf">am5b12242_si_001.pdf (465.72 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b12242" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </p><ol id="references" class="useLabel"><li id="ref1"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">1</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit1" data-doi="10.1126/science.1212741"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dunn, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kamath, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Science</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">334</span><span class="NLM_x"> (</span><span class="NLM_issue">6058</span><span class="NLM_x">) </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">928</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">935</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1126/science.1212741</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D334%26publication_year%3D2011%26pages%3D928-935%26journal%3DScience%26issue%3D6058%26author%3DB.%2BDunn%26author%3DH.%2BKamath%26author%3DJ.-M.%2BTarascon%26title%3DElectrical%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2Bfor%2Bthe%2BGrid%253A%2BA%2BBattery%2Bof%2BChoices%26doi%3D10.1126%252Fscience.1212741&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.5b12242&amp;doiOfLink=10.1126%2Fscience.1212741&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dunn, Bruce; Kamath, Haresh; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">334</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6058</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">928-935</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Nanoparticle Electrodes For Aqueous Sodium and Potassium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wessells, Colin D.; Peddada, Sandeep V.; Huggins, Robert A.; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The elec. power grid faces a growing need for large-scale energy storage over a wide range of time scales due to costly short-term transients, frequency regulation, and load balancing.  The durability, high power, energy efficiency, and low cost needed for grid-scale storage pose substantial challenges for conventional battery technol.  Here, we demonstrate insertion/extn. of sodium and potassium ions in a low-strain nickel hexacyanoferrate electrode material for at least five thousand deep cycles at high current densities in inexpensive aq. electrolytes.  Its open-framework structure allows retention of 66% of the initial capacity even at a high (41.7C) rate.  At low current densities, its round trip energy efficiency reaches 99%.  This low-cost material is readily synthesized in bulk quantities.  The long cycle life, high power, good energy efficiency, safety, and inexpensive prodn. method make nickel hexacyanoferrate an attractive candidate for use in large-scale batteries to support the elec. grid.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGob9BQIKrnAjrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHiXIDOqvS_g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtl2ktL7J&amp;md5=d1ee3adb1a2dd638a729df1cfdf03be8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seo, D.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHiXIDOqvS_g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, S.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgHiXIDOqvS_g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.2085"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Larcher, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Bismuth: A new anode for the Na-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Dawei; Dou, Shixue; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">88-95</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Bismuth nanoparticles wrapped by graphene have been synthesized.  Refined X-ray diffraction, SEM, and transmission electron microscopy analyses have revealed the phase, crystal structure, and morphol. of the Bi@graphene nanocomposite.  When applied as anode in Na-ion batteries, Bi@graphene nanocomposite exhibited a high reversible sodium storage capacity of about 561 mA h g-1 within the 2.0-0.01 V voltage range and 358 mA h g-1 within the 0.9-0.3 V voltage range.  Ex-situ X-ray diffraction measurements were used to study the reaction mechanism with Na.  It was found that bismuth does not follow the alloying mechanism with Na, and surprisingly, an intercalation process has been evidenced.  The as-prepd. Bi@graphene nanocomposite also demonstrated excellent high rate performance.  This superior electrochem. performance could be ascribed to the unique layered crystal structure of Bi, which has large interlayer spacing along the c-axis (d(003)=3.95 Å) to accommodate the Na ions.  Furthermore, the three-dimensional architecture of the Bi@graphene nanocomposite also contributes to better cond. and stability of the electrodes.  Via d. function theory calcns., it was found that the Bi could provide facile sites for Na ion diffusion and accommodation, based on the intercalation mechanism instead of the alloying process.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplX7y13KbwIrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj6XadDFlKBbA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitFyrsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=69d309a7dadeae452f95810d426e3b29</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta11568b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Baggetto, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ganesh, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, C.-N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Meisner, R.
